Elevator  Maintenance  4,180-1

## CHAPTER  158A

Elevator  Maintenance

Elevator  maintenance  may  be  defined  as  the  work  of  caring
for  the  elevator  plant  or  equipment  in  such  a  manner  that  all  parts
of  the  system  will  function  properly  and  continue  to  do  so  year
in  and  year  out  without  undue  interruption  of  service.

There  are  three  methods  generally  employed  to  obtain  ele-
vator maintenance.  They  are:

1.  To  contract  for  the  entire  maintenance  program  with

an  elevator  company;

2.  To  make  an  arrangement  with  an  elevator  company  to

send  out  men  for  inspection  and  service  at  regular
time  intervals;

3.  To  employ  expert  maintenance  men  in  the  building

proper  and  purchase  repair  parts  only  as  the  need
arises.

In  the  first  case  the  elevator  company  assumes  full  responsi-
bility for  everything,  such  as  supplies,  repairs,  cable  replace-
ment, trouble  calls,  etc. — night  and  day.

The  second  method  means  keeping  an  elevator  man  in  con-
stant attendance,  and  is  obviously  one  which  may  vary  through
a  considerable  range  according  to  what  proportion  of  work  is
to  be  done  by  the  elevator  company  under  their  service  contract.

4, 180-2 Elevator  Maintenance

The  third  method  implies  the  employment  of  expert  service
mechanics,  the  personnel  depending  on  the  number  of  cars  in
use  and  to  some  extent  on  the  kind  of  elevator  equipment.

No  attempt  will  be  made  here  to  discuss  the  relative  merits  of  these  three
methods,  because  generally  each  case  will  be  governed  by  its  own  merit.
Many  owners,  however,  prefer  the  third  method  because  it  enables  the
owner  to  know  exactly  in  advance  what  the  elevator  item  on  the  year's
maintenance  budget  will  be;  second,  because  they  are  assured  of  expert  up-
keep and  adjustment,  and  third,  because  they  are  in  effect  insured  against
unusual  repair  bills.

Elevator  Tests. — On  the  completion  of  an  installation  of
electric  elevators,  tests  are  made  to  see  that  all  parts  of  the
equipment  are  in  good  working  order;  that  the  speed  and  load
requirements  have  been  met,  and  that  the  safety  equipment  can
be  relied  upon  satisfactorily  to  operate  as  and  when  required.
The  principal  points  that  receive  attention  are  outlined  as
follows:

A  careful  inspection  of  all  details  of  the  elevator  plant  should  be  made
to  see  that  the  contract  has  been  executed  in  a  thoroughly  satisfactory  and
workmanlike  manner.  Points  to  note  are  conduit  and  cable;  main  switches
and  fuses;  control  panel;  leads  from  control  panel  to  motor,  etc.;  machine
bed;  foundation  bolts;  bed  plate  level  in  both  directions;  that  all  bolts  and
nuts  are  tight;  gears  (if  used)  and  bearings  supplied  with  oil;  rope  fastenings
and  groove  conditions;  floor  leveling  device;  guide  rails;  fixings  and  joints;
car;  car  operating  device;  signaling  system;  landing  doors,  electrical  inter-
locks and  wiring  for  same;  counterweights  and  rope  connections,  etc.

Weights  equivalent  to  the  rated  car  capacity  is  placed  in  the  car  and
speed  checked,  allowing  for  acceleration  and  retardation.  Full  speed
checked  on  both  upward  and  downward  journeys  should  not  vary  by  more
than  15%.

Limit  on  tests  are  made  with  a  load  of  150  pounds  (operator)  only  in  the
car.  The  figure  obtained  for  speeds  with  minimum  and  maximum  loads
in  the  car  are  then  compared.

Acceleration  and  retardation  are  checked  with  minimum  and  maximum
loads  in  the  car  to  see  that  it  is  smooth  and  even  under  both  conditions  and
in  both  directions  of  travel.

Elevator  Maintenance 4,180-3

The  test  required  to  prove  the  car  safety  gear  and  the  guide  grips  will
vary  with  the  type  installed.

Ride  in  the  car  and  observe  if  there  is  any  objectionable  side  or  end  play,
due  to  incorrectly  adjusted  guide  shoes.

With  full  load  in  the  car,  run  the  car  at  full  speed  into  the  limit  switches,
both  at  the  top  and  at  the  bottom  of  the  shaft.  Then  cut  out  the  limit
switches  and  repeat  the  test  with  the  ultimate  limit  or  overtravel  switches
only.

With  full  load  in  the  car,  observe  the  starting  kick  and  running  current
and  check  this  with  the  rating  plate  on  the  motor.  Using  a  dead  beat
ammeter ,  allow  5  %  kick  or  final  rush .  The  accelerating  current  should  not
exceed  50%  in  normal  cases  on  a  direct  current  motor  and  250%  on  an
alternating  current  motor.

Gradually  reduce  the  load  in  the  car  until  the  current  on  the  up  trip  is
equal  to  the  current  on  the  down  trip.  At  this  point  the  load  in  the  car
should  normally  equal  40%  of  the  rated  car  capacity.

Check  the  operation  of  all  safety  interlocks  fitted  to  landing  floors.

Run  the  car  for  a  mile  at  capacity  load  and  observe  acceleration,  retarda-
tion, and  also  any  excessive  noise  or  vibration.  Immediately  afterwards
switch  off  the  current  and  observe  the  temperature  of  all  bearings,  gears,
motors,  controllers,  brakes,  resistances,  etc.

Take  insulation  tests  of  motion  controllers  and  wiring  and  localize  cause
of  low  readings  (if  any) .

Carefully  check  the  operation  of  the  signaling  equipment.

The  maintenance  engineer  should  understand  all  of  the  foregoing  thor-
oughly, and  he  should  have  spare  parts  for  all  points  of  the  elevator.  A
card  of  "Working  Instructions"  should  be  securely  fixed  in  the  motor  room
or  other  convenient  place,  bearing  the  name  and  telephone  number  of  the
nearest  Elevator  Service  Depot.

Character  of  Inspection* — The  character  of  service  rendered
in  most  installations  is  a  weekly  examination  of  the  entire
plant,  except  signal  devices,  including  oiling  and  cleaning  the
machine,  motor  and  controller,  greasing  and  oiling  bearings  and
guides,  making  necessary  minor  adjustments  and  furnishing
call-back  service  during  regular  time.  This  service  also  in-
cludes the  furnishing  of  small  parts  for  replacements,  such  as

4,180-4 Elevator  Maintenance

copper  and  carbon  contacts,  insulation,  springs,  braids,  motor
brushes;  also  oil  and  grease.

Where  high-speed  elevators  are  operating  under  severe  service
requirements,  it  is  often  necessary  to  have  examinations  and
adjustments  made  daily.  The  examinations  may  be  made
semi-monthly,  where  the  service  is  intermittent.

A  well-organized  scientific  inspection  embracing  the  following
features  with  the  object  of  constantly  maintaining  an  elevator
at  the  highest  point  of  efficiency  and  safety  should  be  organized.
This  includes:

1.  An  expert  regular  examination  of  the  apparatus;

2.  The  thorough  lubrication  of  the  machinery  with  the

best  lubricants;

3.  A  regular  adjustment  of  the  moving  parts;

4.  The  renewal  of  all  wearing  parts  prior  to  any  chance  of

their  failing  to  function;

5.  The  careful  examination  of  cables  and  their  renewal

before  the  probability  of  breakage;

6.  Renewal  or  repair  of  all  other  parts  of  the  elveator

whenever  such  is  required.

This  service,  which  numbers  its  users  by  the  thousands,  acts
as  a  preventative  for  troubles  of  all  kinds,  and  provides  the
elevator  owner  with  many  advantages,  such  as:

1.  He   can   determine   beforehand   a   reasonably   fixed

monthly  cost  for  maintaining  his  elevators.  As  this
is  in  the  nature  of  cost  insurance,  it  enables  him  to
budget  accurately;

2.  He  can  offer  to  the  passengers  of  his  elevators  (the

public)  the  maximum  of  safety,  together  with  the
absence  of  those  annoying  delays  due  to  interruption
in  the  service;

Elevator  Maintenance 4,180-5

3.  It  materially  prolongs  the  life  of  the  apparatus  and  pre-
vents later  costly  repairs  or  even  complete  renewals.

This  service  should  be  handled  by  a  special  expert  examina-
tion that  functions  in  the  interest  of  elevator  users.

A  regular  system  of  inspection  is  one  of  the  essentials  to
rendering  good  elevator  service  and  keeping  the  equipments  in
a  safe  operating  condition.

Where  to  begin  inspecting  elevator  equipment  is  a  question  that  engineers
are  not  agreed  upon.  Where  some  will  start  with  the  motor,  others  will
begin  with  the  hoistway  doors  or  start  on  hoisting  ropes.  It  makes  little
difference  where  the  work  is  started  so  long  as  it  is  thorough.

Starting  at  the  motor,  the  lubrication,  the  condition  of  the  bearings,  the
brushes  and  commutator,  and  any  evidence  of  winding  trouble  are  noted.
On  worm-gear  machines,  the  position  of  the  armature  or  rotor  between  the
bearings  should  be  checked.  When  the  thrust  bearings  wear,  they  mav
allow  the  thrust  of  the  worm  shaft  to  be  transferred  to  the  motor  bearing.
The  latter  are  not  designed  to  take  the  thrust  and  they  may  overheat  if
subjected  to  it.

Next  to  the  motor  comes  the  brake.  To  a  degree,  on  its  adjustment  and
condition  depends  the  safety  of  the  passengers  in  the  car.  If  it  is  not  work-
ing properly,  the  operator  will  make  many  false  stops.  These  not  only
impair  the  elevator  service,  but  also  increase  the  power  consumption  per
car  mile.  Therefore,  safety,  service  and  economy  dictate  that  the  brake
should  be  properly  adjusted.

The  control  equipment  may  be  given  a  third  place  on  the  program.  All
contacts  should  be  examined  to  ascertain  that  they  are  functioning  properly.
There  are  many  things  that  can  happen  on  a  controller,  such  as  contacts
out  of  adjustment,  shunts  and  contacts  loose  or  broken,  all  of  which  should
be  located  and  repaired  before  they  cause  trouble.

While  in  the  motor  room  the  elevator  machine  should  be  checked.  If
the  machine  is  of  the  gear  type,  check  is  made  of  the  height  of  the  oil  in
the  gear  case.  Its  level  should  be  at  the  center  line  of  the  worm.  The
worm  should  not  have  excessive  thrust.  Before  this  condition  exists,  the
thrust  bearings  should  be  adjusted  or  renewed.  Lost  motion  on  the  drum
is  usually  due  to  one  or  more  of  the  following  reasons:  worn  thrust  bearings,
worn  gear  teeth,  loose  or  worn  bearings  and  the  gear  loose  on  the  spider.
All  the  causes  should  be  checked  and  corrected  if  found  to  exist.

!,i80-6  Elevator  Maintenance

The  only  remedy  for  a  badly  worn  gear  is  to  replace  it.  The  drum  and
sheaves  should  be  checked  for  looseness  on  their  shafts.  Their  rims  and
spokes  should  be  examined  and  hammer  tested  for  cracks.

The  ropes  on  the  drum  and  sheaves  should  be  given  attention  to  see  that
they  lead  off  and  on  the  drum  without  bending.  Where  deflecting  sheaves
are  used  on  drum  machines,  they  should  move  freely  on  their  shafts  or  they
will  cause  the  rppes  to  rub  on  the  side  of  the  grooves  and  result  in  unnec-
essary wear.

When  inspecting  the  governor,  the  flyballs  are  lifted  until  the  rope
gripping  jaws  are  released,  noting  if  the  auxiliary  switch  functions  properly.
The  governor  should  be  reset;  care  being  taken  to  see  that  the  auxiliary
switches  are  in  their  correct  positions.  The  governor  should  be  kept  clean
and  all  moving  parts  lubricated.

After  the  equipment  in  the  motor  room  has  been  inspected,  the  hoistway
and  the  car  should  be  given  attention.  The  hoistway  doors  and  their  locks
should  be  carefully  gone  over.  Where  the  doors  are  held  closed  by  ordinary
latches,  the  inspector  should  try  to  open  each  door  without  lifting  its  latch.
It  should  also  be  noted  if  the  doors  latch  readily  when  they  are  closed.

When  the  doors  have  interlocks,  the  inspector  should  try  to  start  the  car
at  each  landing  with  the  door  partly  open.  If  an  automatic  door  closer  is
used,  it  should  be  noted  how  far  the  doors  are  open  when  the  interlock
closes  and  the  car  can  be  started .  If  the  doors  are  power-operated ,  all  parts
of  the  operating  mechanism  must  be  examined  carefully  to  see  that  it  is  in
proper  working  order.

The  safety  devices  under  the  car  should  be  examined  by  the  inspector,
getting  down  into  the  pit  and  having  the  operator  lower  the  car  to  expose
all  parts.  The  guide  shoes  should  be  replaced  before  they  are  worn  suffi-
ciently to  allow  the  safety  shoes  to  come  in  contact  with  the  rails.  The
safety  devices  should  be  operated  by  hand  to  insure  that  they  are  in  good
working  condition,  and  that  all  moving  parts  are  well  lubricated.  While
the  inspector  is  in  the  pit  he  should  examine  the  oil  buffers  to  see  that  they
contain  sufficient  oil.

When  the  car  has  compensating  ropes,  they  should  be  examined  from  the
pit.  The  best  way  to  do  this  is  first  to  examine  the  connections  at  the  car
to  see  that  they  are  secure  and  that  there  are  no  broken  wires  at  the  sockets.
Then,  while  the  operator  raises  the  car  about  three  feet  at  a  time,  the  in-
spector examines  the  ropes  for  broken  wires  or  other  objectionable  condi-
tions. When  the  car  has  reached  the  top  of  the  hoistway,  if  the  counter-
weights come  down  far  enough,  the  rope's  connections  to  their  bottom  can
be  examined,  as  can  the  lower  guide  shoes.  If  this  inspection  cannot  be
made  from  here ,  it  can  be  taken  care  of  from  the  top  of  the  car .  The  length

Elevator  Maintenance  4,180-7

of  the  ropes  should  be  noted .  With  the  car  at  the  top  landing,  there  should
be  one  or  two  feet  clearance  before  the  counterweights  will  land  on  the
buffer  in  the  pit.

The  position  of  the  governor  rope  and  the  compensating  rope  sheaves
should  be  noted  to  see  that  they  are  in  a  position  to  function  properly  and
have  sufficient  clearance  under  them.

The  hoisting  ropes  and  governor  ropes  can  best  be  inspected  from  the
top  of  the  car,  with  an  inspector  on  top,  the  operator  running  the  car  to  a
position  in  the  hoistway  where  the  counterweights  can  be  reached.  These
can  be  inspected  first,  and  then  as  the  operator  raises  the  car,  about  two
feet  at  a  time,  the  inspector  should  carefully  examine  the  ropes.  The  car
guide  shoes,  limit  switches  and  other  equipment  that  may  be  on  top  of  the
car  and  in  the  hoistway  should  be  examined  as  part  of  the  rope-inspecting
job.

In  the  car,  the  chief  objects  of  attention  are  the  car  switch  and  the  gate.
The  safety  switch  should  be  opened  and  the  car  switch  moved  to  the  on
position  to  see  if  the  potential  switch  drops  out  and  cuts  the  power  off  the
motor,  as  it  should.  The  cover  is  next  removed  from  the  car  switch  and
the  contacts  inspected  to  see  if  they  are  in  good  condition  and  function
correctly.  If  the  gate  has  a  contact  to  prevent  the  car's  being  started  with
it  open,  a  search  should  be  made  for  the  cause  of  the  short  circuit  on  the
contact  circuit.

The  equipment  to  be  inspected  and  the  method  of  doing  it  will  vary  with
the  type  of  machine,  but  the  foregoing  suggests  a  system  that  covers  the
most  important  features  of  any  installation.  In  the  maintenance  of
elevators,  like  with  any  other  equipment,  keeping  it  clean  is  about  75%  of
the  job.  If  equipment  is  thoroughly  cleaned  every  week  or  two  most  of
the  minor  defects  will  be  detected.

It  is  important  to  remove  all  kinks  from  the  governor  and  safety-operat-
ing rope;  also  to  examine  the  governor  rope  to  determine  if  it  has  been
damaged  sufficiently  to  require  the  installation  of  a  new  rope.

If  the  elevator  is  equipped  with  a  roll-type  safety,  the  rollers  in  the  safety
jaws  should  be  inspected  after  an  application  of  the  safety,  before  the  el-
evator is  again  started,  to  make  sure  that  the  rollers  are  in  their  correct
position  for  the  next  application.

After  any  car  safety  application  and  before  the  car  is  again  placed  in
operation,  a  thorough  inspection  should  be  made  of  the  governor,  tension
frame  and  all  parts  of  the  safety  mechanism.

4,180-8 Elevator  Maintenance

On  drum  machines  it  is  very  important  that  all  slack  in  the  hoisting
ropes  be  taken  up  before  the  safety  is  released;  otherwise  the  car  will  drop
until  the  slack  is  taken  up  and  the  sudden  stop  may  snap  the  ropes.  The
slack  is  taken  up  by  slowly  turning  over  the  machine  until  the  ropes  are
taut.  Be  sure  that  the  ropes  go  back  on  the  drum  in  the  proper  grooves.

Some  safe-lift  drum-type  machines  are  equipped  with  back-gears,  and
are  provided  with  additional  counterweights.  On  single- wrap  traction  and
gearless  traction  safe  lift  elevators,  there  is  provided  additional  counter-
weight and  a  throw-over  switch  on  the  controller  to  short  circuit  a  portion
of  the  starting  resistance .  On  some  micro-drive  gearless  elevators  a  throw-
over  switch  is  provided  to  permit  continuous  operation  at  slow  speed  by
means  of  the  leveling  motion.  Before  attempting  to  carry  safes  or  other
heavy  material  on  the  car,  the  extra  counterweight  (where  provided)  should
be  added,  and  the  machine  arranged  for  safe-lift  operation  in  increasing  the
brake  spring  tension  on  both  brakes.

All  safe-lift  elevators  are  also  equipped  with  a  locking  bar  on  each  end
of  the  safety  frame.  When  these  bars  are  thrown  out  in  their  extreme
position,  they  land  on  clips  on  the  guide  rail  at  each  floor.  This  locking
device  is  operated  by  a  wrench  through  the  corner  of  the  car  platform.

Before  operating  the  locking  bars,  move  the  car  platform  a  few  inches
above  the  designated  floor,  and  by  means  of  the  socket  wrench  throw  the
bars  out  in  their  extreme  position.  Then  lower  the  car  very  slowly  until
the  platform  comes  to  rest  securely  on  the  clips.

Be  sure  that  the  bars  have  a  full  bearing  on  the  clips.  After  loading  the
safe  on  the  car,  raise  the  car  a  few  inches,  and  by  means  of  the  socket
wrench  move  the  bars  to  their  extreme  position.  Be  sure  that  the  bars  are
clean  of  the  clips.  Under  no  circumstances  attempt  to  load  a  safe  or  other
heavy  object  on  the  elevator  platform  without  landing  the  car  platform
on  the  clips  as  above  noted,  and  after  loading  the  platform  do  not  attempt
to  lower  or  raise  the  load,  except  as  noted,  until  the  bars  have  been  with-
drawn and  are  entirely  clear  of  the  dips.

The  largest  item  of  maintenance  cost  of  a  modern  high  speed  elevator  is
the  ropes.  Operating  conditions  have  a  marked  influence  on  rope  life.
Some  of  these  are  controllable,  others  are  not.  In  a  given  installation  un-
equal loading  is  probably  one  of  the  chief  causes  of  short  rope  life.  On  a
traction  machine  this  may  develop  from  unequal  stretch  of  the  rope  or  un-
equal tread  diameter,  or  the  grooves  in  the  traction  sheave  will  cause  one
rope  to  travel  faster  than  the  other,  producing  a  slippage  and  unequal
loading.

Elevator  Maintenance  4,180-9

Devices  such  as  commutator  stones  and  grinders,  commutator  under-
cutting tools  and  hand  motor-driven  blowers  have  a  place  in  the  main-
tenance of  the  electric  equipment  on  elevators  just  as  they  have  on  electrical
machinery  for  industrial  purposes.

Once  each  year,  or  every  two  years,  depending  on  conditions,  the  motor
windings  should  be  given  a  thorough  cleaning  and  a  coat  of  air  drying
insulation  varnish.

Lubricating  the  guide  rails  by  hand  has  always  been  not  only  an  un-
pleasant job,  but  also  an  unsatisfactory  way  of  doing  work.  When  they
are  first  greased  there  is  too  much  lubricant  on  them,  and  before  they  are
greased  again  they  are  frequently  dry.  Several  automatic  guide  rail
lubricators  have  been  developed  that  reduce  the  job  to  filling  a  reservoir
with  oil  or  grease  every  month ,  or  at  longer  intervals .  These  devices  relieve
the  maintenance  man  of  an  objectionable  and  dangerous  job,  insures  the
rails  getting  a  continuous  supply  of  lubricant,  and  the  car  does  not  have  to
be  taken  out  of  service  to  grease  rails.

Metering  devices  are  valuable  tools  in  a  proper  operating  and  main-
tenance program.  A  megohm  meter  should  be  used  to  check  the  electrical
machinery  insulation.  A  watt-hour  meter  on  each  elevator  in  connection
with  a  mileage  recorder  are  used  to  obtain  a  record  of  power  consumption
per  car  mile .  They  show  the  daily  mileage  of  each  car  and  rope  1  if e .  When
stop  recorders  or  counters  are  used  on  car  switch-controlled  elevators,  their
record  combined  with  those  of  the  mileage  recorders  will  provide  a  means  of
obtaining  the  stops  per  car  mile  and  per  trip.  This  is  valuable  in  checking
up  on  operators  and  the  brake  adjustment.

If  an  operator  is  making  an  excessive  number  of  stops,  it  shows  that
many  of  them  are  false,  either  through  the  fault  of  the  operator  or  because
of  an  improperly  adjusted  brake.  A  study  of  records  of  the  number  of
stops  per  car  mile  and  of  power  consumption  shows  the  power  per  car  mile
invariably  increases  as  the  number  of  stops  increase.

Modern  elevator  equipment  has  been  brought  to  such  a  high  degree  oi
development  that  its  expected  life  can  be  assumed  to  be  that  of  the  building.
However,  there  are  many  conditions  in  the  older  type  of  buildings  where
changes  can  be  made  that  will  improve  elevator  service.  Even  modern
elevators  may  be  made  more  efficient  and  the  service  improved  by  changes
in  the  auxiliary  equipment.  The  old  signal  system  may  be  replaced  by
one  that  is  more  effective;  an  automatic  scheduling  system  may  be  installed
to  dispatch  the  cars  instead  of  having  this  done  by  the  starter;  manually
operated  doors  may  be  equipped  with  automatic  closers  or  they  may  be
power-operated  and  an  improvement  in  the  service  obtained.

4,180-10  Elevator  Maintenance

In  the  older  types  of  buildings  many  changes  have  been  made  by  install-
ing complete  elevator  equipment  Frequently  old  controllers  are  replaced .
Where  the  machines  are  operated  from  a.c.  it  is  often  possible  to  replace  the
old  motor  by  a  new  one  and  reduce  the  power  consumption  by  one-half,
improve  the  elevator  service  and  reduce  the  maintenance  cost,  thereby
effecting  considerable  saving.

Servicing  Elevators

The  following  schedule  in  servicing  will  greatly  facilitate  the
work  of  servicing,  as  follows:

Operation  No.  1. — Examine  main  line  switch  serving  elevator  to  insure
that  switch  blades  are  working  properly  and  that  proper  size  fuses  are  used.
Also  see  that  proper  fuses  are  used  on  controller  and  that  they  are  making
proper  contact  in  holders.

Operation  No,  2.— Examine  and  adjust  all  moving  parts  of  the  con-
troller. Clean  or  renew  contacts  where  needed  and  check  up  to  see  if  all
connections  on  binding  posts  are  secure.  Hinge  pins  and  other  parts  which
require  it  should  be  lubricated.

All  connections  to  motor  should  be  examined  to  insure  that  they  are  se-
cure and  in  proper  place.  If  of  the  slip  ring  or  commutator  type  the
brushes,  brush  holders,  commutator,  slip  rings  and  other  parts  having  to
do  with  the  motor's  operation  should  be  gone  over  carefully  to  insure  that
they  are  in  proper  condition  and  adjustment,

In  the  case  of  a.c.  motor,  either  for  the  elevator  proper  or  the  M.G.  set,
the  air  gap  should  be  checked  to  insure  that  it  is  correct .  All  motor  wind-
ings, irrespective  of  the  type,  should  be  gone  over  carefully  to  insure  that
they  are  free  from  oil  and  dirt  and  other  foreign  matter  which  might  result
in  damaging  such  parts.

The  bearings,  all  motors,  generators,  etc.,  should  be  gone  over  very
carefully  to  insure  that  there  is  sufficient  oil  to  properly  lubricate  them  and
that  the  oil  is  of  the  proper  quality.  Such  bearings  should  be  drained  off
at  proper  intervals  and  fresh  oil  supplied.

Operation  No.  3. — Examine  brake  to  insure  that  connections  to  magnet
coils  or  motor  are  secure  and  that  all  adjustments  are  correct  for  the  service,
especially  that  the  brake  shoes  are  adjusted  as  close  to  the  pulley  as  con-
ditions will  permit.  All  pins  and  other  moving  parts  should  be  lubricated
and  a  careful  examination  should  be  made  of  all  such  parts  to  insure  that
cotter  pins  or  any  other  method  which  is  used  to  hold  such  parts  are  in
proper  place.

Elevator  Maintenance  4,180-11

In  case  of  an  ax.  brake,  the  proper  amount  and  quality  of  oil  should  be
kept  in  the  magnet  case.  This  should  be  looked  after  at  each  examination.

In  the  case  of  an  a.c.  motor-operated  brake,  be  sure  that  the  dash  pot
adjustment  is  correct.

Operation  No.  4. — The  lubrication  of  the  worm  and  gear  should  be
such  as  to  insure  that  the  latter  carries  oil  over  the  top.  Usually  keeping"
the  worm  chamber  two-thirds  full  will  accomplish  this.  The  worm-gear
chamber  should  be  drained  out  at  proper  intervals,  depending  on  the  service
of  the  elevator  and  fresh  oil  supplied.

The  lubrication  and  condition  of  all  drum  or  driving  sheave  shaft  bearings-
should  be  checked  carefully,  also  if  there  is  excessive  play  in  the  worm  or
other  shafts,  the  stuffing  box  gland  should  be  adjusted  to  insure  that  an
excessive  amount  of  oil  will  not  leak  out.

Operation  No.  5. — Examine  carefully  the  safety  governor  to  insure
that  all  parts  are  in  proper  condition  and  well  lubricated.  This  is  very
-important.

Operation  No.  6. — Clean  machine,  governor,  controller,  motor  and
all  other  parts.  Especially,  see  that  the  machine  room  is  keot  free  from  oil
cans,  dirty  waste  and  all  other  rubbish.  Oily  or  dirty  waste  should  in  no
case  be  left  exposed  or  thrown  where  it  might  cause  damage  from  fire.  It
is  possible  for  oil-soaked  waste  to  cause  spontaneous  combustion.  For
this  reason  it  should  be  kept  in  a  metal  container  or  disposed  of  in  such  a
way  as  to  avoid  any  possible  damage  from  this  action.

Operation  No.  7. — Examine  carefully  all  ropes  to  insure  that  there  are
no  broken  wires,  damaged  places  or  other  conditions  which  might  result  in
their  breakage  and  cause  a  serious  accident.  Ropes  as  a  rule  give  some
warning  of  their  condition  before  it  is  necessary  to  renew  them.  It  is
possible  for  wire  ropes  to  break  up  internally  if  not  properly  lubricated.
For  this  reason  all  old  ropes  should  be  lubricated.

When  ropes  are  manufactured  the  center  core  is  saturated  with  a  lubri-
cating compound.  When  the  rope  is  put  into  service  this  lubrication  pene-
trates the  wires  and  it  will  keep  them  lubricated  for  some  time.  However,
this  lubrication  is  gradually  used  up  and  even  though  the  rope  appears  to
be  properly  lubricated  on  the  outside,  it  is  possible  that  the  fibre  core  in  the
center  of  the  rope  and  the  wires  around  it  are  dry  and  cutting.  The  service
of  the  elevator  and  the  condition  of  the  rope  itself  will  determine  the  amount
of  lubrication  required,

For  basement  machine,  all  overhead  sheave  bearings,  shafts  and  sheave
grooves  should  be  gone  over  carefully  and  the  former  properly  lubricated.

4,180-12  Elevator  Maintenance

The  importance  of  keeping  overhead  sheave  bearings  or  in  fact  slow  moving
journals  of  all  kinds  properly  lubricated  is  very  often  overlooked.

The  fact  that  there  is  grease  in  the  chamber  provided  in  the  bearing  does
not  insure  that  the  journal  is  lubricated.  Grease  will  become  hard  if
allowed  indefinitely  to  remain  in  grease  cups  or  other  reservoirs  which  have
been  provided  for  lubrication.  It  is  necessary  from  time  to  time  to  clean
out  the  old  grease,  also  the  oil  holes  leading  to  the  journals,  to  insure  that
the  fresh  oil  which  is  being  supplied  will  do  its  work.  Lack  of  lubrication
at  such  points  will  result  in  unnecessary  friction  and  excessive  wear.

Operation  No.  8. — Examine  main  and  counterweight  rails  to  see  that
all  brackets  and  rail  clips  are  tight;  also  that  rails  are  properly  lubricated.
Check  up  on  car  shoes  for  wear  and  adjustment  and  see  if  they  are  properly
secured  to  crosshead  and  safety  plank.  Where  compensating  chains  are
used,  examine  chains  and  hitches  on  outriggers  and  final  hitches  on  safety
plant  and  hitches  on  bottom  of  counterweight  frame  to  see  if  there  is
any  indication  of  their  breaking  loose.

If  wire  ropes  are  used  for  compensation,  see  that  they  are  properly
adjusted  for  tension  and  hitches  under  car,  and  that  bottom  of  counter-
weight frame  is  secure.  Also  check  for  wearing.  Examine  compensating
sheave  bearings  for  condition  and  lubrication;  also  see  that  frame  is  free  in
guides  and  that  slack  cable  switch  for  same  is  working  properly .  Examine
governor  tension  sheave  and  make  sure  that  it  is  free  and  well  lubricated.
If  oil  buffers  are  used  under  car  and  counterweight  see  that  they  are  in
proper  working  order  and  oil  chambers  are  kept  filled  to  the  proper  level
with  the  correct  oil.

Operation  No.  9. — Examine  safety  devices.  If  roll  safety  is  used,
make  sure  that  tripping  rods  are  set  even  so  that  the  steel  rollers  in  safety
shoes  will  rise  at  the  same  time;  otherwise  if  one  engages  before  the  other,
the  car  will  pawl  out  of  level  and  may  cause  serious  damage.  See  that  ail
moving  parts  of  safety  devices  are  free  and  well  lubricated.

If  wedge  clamp  safety  is  used,  examine  releasing  carrier  and  lead  sheaves
for  safety  rope  and  make  sure  that  there  is  no  undue  friction  on  sheaves;
also  that  they  are  properly  lubricated.  Examine  safety  drum  rope  to  see
that  there  are  no  kinks  on  safety  drum;  also  that  cable  is  set  properly  in
grooves  of  safety  drum.  Make  sure  that  safety  drum  is  free  and  all  bearings
and  moving  parts  are  well  lubricated.  If  flexible  guide  clamp  safety  is
used,  examine  lifting  rods  for  safety  rollers  in  safety  shoes  and  see  that  they
lift  even,  so  that  car  platform  will  be  level  when  safety  is  applied;  that
safety  releasing  spring  is  properly  set;  that  equalizing  spring  is  properly  set
to  give  proper  clearance  to  jaws  on  rails.  Also,  that  nuts  for  releasing
safety  jaws  are  backed  free  from  jaws  after  safety  is  released .  Examine  all

Elevator  Maintenance  4,180-13

moving  parts  of  safety  and  see  that  they  are  properly  adjusted  and  well
lubricated.  Examine  all  bolts  in  carframe  and  counterweight  frame  and
tie  rods  for  counterweights.  If  car  emergency  safety  device  is  used,  see
that  all  the  moving  parts  are  kept  free  and  well  lubricated.  This  operation
is  very  important.

Operation  No.  10. — Examine  traveling  cables  to  center  junction  boxes
and  under  car  platform  to  see  if  steel  supporting  wires  are  holding.  Exam-
ine traveling  cables  throughouc  shaft  to  see  if  outside  coverings  are  wearing
off;  also,  if  traveling  cables  are  fouling  anywhere  in  shaft.  Examine  top
and  bottom  final  shaftway  limit  switches  and  make  sure  that  they  operate
properly.  If  the  different  switches  for  the  up  and  down  direction  of  car
travel  are  mounted  in  shaft  or  on  crosshead  of  car,  see  that  contacts  for
these  different  switches  are  making  properly;  also  that  moving  parts  of
these  switches  are  lubricated  where  needed.

See  that  cams  for  these  switches  are  secure  either  to  car  or  rails.  Check
the  up  and  down  direction  of  car  travel  from  car  switch  to  see  if  automatic
device  operates  properly;  also,  see  if  car  switch  contacts  are  making  properly,
and  contacts  and  other  moving  parts  of  switch  are  well  lubricated;  renew
contacts  in  all  switches  where  needed.  Examine  safety  switch  in  car  and
see  that  contacts  are  making  properly;  also,  examine  safety  plank  switch
under  platform  of  car.

Examine  door  contacts  and  gate  contacts,  adjusting  or  renewing  parts
where  necessary;  also,  examine  emergency  cutout  switches  for  door  and
gate  contacts.  Examine  light  switch  and  fixture  in  car  and  see  that  they
are  in  proper  working  order.  Examine  and  adjust  door-closers  if  used  and
included  in  service  contract,

Operation  No.  11. — Clean  top  and  inside  of  car  under  platform,  main
and  counterweight  rails  and  bracks  in  shaft,  counterweight  frame  and  pit
of  elevator  shaft,  ledges,  sills  and  other  hatch  projections.

Operation  No.  12. — Examiner  should  have  his  report  signed  by  someone
in  authority  at  the  building.  Also  make  out  report  for  any  recommenda-
tions on  regular  form,  not  on  his  report  sheet.

General. — The  foregoing  system  can  be  applied  to  all  types
of  elevators  with  the  following  exceptions:

Hand  Rope  Operation. — Examine  operating  rope  for  wear;
also,  see  that  top  and  bottom  terminal  stop  balls  are  properly
set;  that  stop  balls  on  back  stop  in  shaft  are  set  to  make  ahead

Elevator  Maintenance

of  steps  on  controller  or  flyer  sheave.  If  drum-type  elevator,
see  that  machine  automatic  device  is  set  for  top  and  bottom
terminal  landings,  also  that  slack  cable  switch  and  parts  are
working  properly.

Push-Button  Operation.—  Examine  contacts  on  floor  con-
troller, adjusting  and  renewing  where  necessary.  Examine
chain  and  sprockets  on  floor  controller  to  see  that  they  are  in
proper  working  order.  See  that  all  moving  parts  are  lubricated
where  needed.  If  single  wrap  traction,  see  that  proper  tension
is  kept  on  the  machine  friction  drive  sprocket  to  properly  oper-
ate floor  controller;  see  that  limit  switch  mounted  on  car  or  in
shaft  that  controls  corrector  mounted  on  floor  controller  is
working  properly;  be  sure  that  terminal  reset  lugs  are  properly
set  on  main  floor  controller  gear;  also  that  friction  stud  chain
and  sprocket  and  gears  are  in  proper  working  order  and  moving
parts  are  lubricated  where  needed.

Check  up  on  floor  landings  to  see  if  platform  is  stopping  level  to  floors,
resetting  where  necessary.

Examine  door  locks  and  contacts  and  cam  magnet  on  motor  that  operates
retiring  cam  for  door  locks,  making  necessary  adjustments  where  needed
or  renewing  contacts  if  needed.

Examine  car  operating  buttons,  hall  buttons,  emergency  stop  button,
emergency  alarm  button  and  bell  ,  light  switch  and  fixture  in  car  to  see  that
they  are  all  in  working  order,  renewing  parts  when  needed.

If  movable  platform  is  used,  see  that  platform  is  free  from  all  undue
friction  and  all  moving  parts  are  well  lubricated;  also  that  king  pin  bolt
is  secure  and  contacts  on  platform  switch  are  secure  and  making  properly.
Proper  action  should  be  checked  very  carefully.

Examine  shaft  enclosure  doors  and  house  locks  to  see  that  they  are  in
proper  working  order.  If  dual  control  is  used,  see  that  throw-over  switch
or  motor-operated  remote  throw-over  switch  mounted  on  controller  are
working  properly.

If  micro  is  used,  see  that  motor-operated  leveling  switch  is  kept  properly
adjusted;  also  contacts  in  same.  Keep  chain  and  friction  device  properly

Elevator  Maintenance  4,180-15

adjusted  and  all  moving  parts  well  lubricated.  Check  up  on  cams  at  each
floor  and  see  if  they  are  properly  set  and  secure;  also  check  up  on  micro
leveling  device  to  see  that  car  platform  stops  level  to  floor  landings.

Micro  Machine. — Examine  gear  shaft  bearings  in  regard  to
lost  motion  in  same;  also  end  play  on  worm  shaft.  See  that
worm  shaft  is  kept  two- thirds  flooded  with  oil  and  that  oil  wells
for  motor  bearings  are  kept  filled  to  the  proper  level.

Unit-Multi- Voltage  Control.— Examine  and  adjust  contacts
on  controller  panel  of  motor  generator  set,  renewing  contacts
where  necessary;  also  check  up  on  all  connections  on  panel  to
see  that  they  are  secure  to  binding  posts.

If  d.c.  examine  armatures  and  fields  of  motor  generator  sets
and  make  sure  all  connections  to  them  are  secure  and  that  com-
mutators are  kept  clean  and  brushes  are  properly  adjusted;
check  to  see  that  oil  wells  of  motor  and  generator  are  kept  full
to  the  proper  level;  also  see  that  contacts  on  governor  switch
are  working  properly  if  switch  is  used  on  set.

When  a.c.  is  used  for  the  power  supply,  examine  connections  to  stator
of  squirrel  cage  induction  motor  and  see  that  they  are  secure;  verify  that
rotor  connections  are  secure;  also  see  that  connections  to  generator  and
exciter  armatures  and  fields  are  secured  and  commutators  are  kept  clean  and
brushes  properly  adjusted  and  oil  wells  for  bearings  kept  filled  to  the  proper
level.

If  main  or  auxiliary  motor  micro  is  used,  see  that  motor-operated  leveling
switch  is  kept  properly  adjusted;  also  that  contacts  are  making  properly.
Keep  chain  and  friction  device  properly  adjusted  and  all  moving  parts  well
lubricated.  Check  up  on  cams  at  each  floor  and  see  if  they  are  properly  set
and  secured.  Also  check  up  on  micro  leveling  device  to  prove  that  car
platform  stops  level  to  floor  landings.

If  auxiliary  motor  micro  is  used,  examine  main  revolving  brake  and  see
that  adjustments  to  it  are  properly  made  and  all  moving  parts  are  well
lubricated;  make  sure  that  brake  for  micro  machine  is  properly  adjusted  and
moving  parts  are  lubricated;  examine  gear  bearing  in  regard  to  lost  motion
on  same;  also  check  up  end  play  on  worm  shafts.  See  that  worm  shaft  is
kept  two-thirds  flooded  with  oil.  Examine  motor  bearings  and  see  that  oil
is  kept  to  proper  level  in  oil  wells.

4,180-16 Elevator  Maintenance

Multi- Voltage  Signal  Control. — Examine  and  adjust  contacts
on  master  signal  controller  board  and  see  that  latching  magnets
are  working  properly  and  that  all  connections  on  front  and  back
of  controller  are  secure  to  binding  posts.

Examine  selector  machine  and  verify  that  traveling  cross-
head  and  revolving  worm  screw  are  free  and  well  lubricated
and  that  all  contact  brushes  and  contact  plates  are  making
properly;  that  all  connections  to  selector  are  secure  to  their
binding  posts;  also  see  that  pawl  magnet  stops  are  properly  °,et.

Examine  selector  sheaves  and  shaft  bearings  and  see  that  they
are  properly  lubricated;  examine  steel  tapes  from  these  sheaves
to  top  of  car  and  to  underside  of  platform;  examine  tape
tension  sheave  in  pit  of  shaft  and  see  that  it  is  free  and  well
lubricated;  that  sprockets  on  end  of  tape  sheave  shaft  and  shaft
to  selector  are  secure;  also  sprocket  chain.  The  brush  magnet
on  crosshead  of  selector  must  be  properly  adjusted  and  con-
tacts for  it  must  make  properly.

Examine  top  and  bottom  of  final  shaftway  limit  switches  and  make  sure
that  they  operate  properly.  Examine  automatic  switch  mounted  on  cross-
head  of  car  and  see  that  it  operates  properly  and  top  and  bottom  stationary
cams  for  same  are  set  properly;  be  certain  that  small  double  series  field  motor
used  for  reversing  purposes  is  free  and  well  lubricated  and  that  all  the  con-
tacts make  properly.

Examine  car-operating  box  and  see  that  the  different  stopping  buttons
are  working  properly;  also  the  starting  switch,  slow  speed  switch,  non-stop
safety  switch,  micro  switch,  emergency  cutout  reversal  switch,  light  switch
and  key  switch  for  starting  and  stopping  the  motor  generator  set .  Examine
safety  plank  switch  mounted  under  car  platform  and  see  that  contacts  are
making  properly.

Examine  door  contacts,  adjusting  or  renewing  contacts  where  necessary,
also  gate  contacts.  Examine  and  adjust  automatic  door  openers  and
closers  to  see  that  they  work  properly;  see  that  light  fixture  is  in  proper
working  order.  Examine  the  up  and  down  hall  signal  lanterns  and  see  that
they  are  in  proper  working  order.  Inspect  up  and  down  buttons,  making
necessary  adjustments  where  needed,  also  renewing  any  contacts  if  necessary.

Gas  and  Electric  Hoists  4,181

## CHAPTER  159

A  hoist  represents  a  class  of  machinery  possessing  wide
utilities,  for  besides  general  hoisting  work,  there  are  other
special  duties  to  which  it  is  adapted.  Its  development  has
been  brought  about  chiefly  by  builders  of  this  class  of  ma-
chinery being  brought  into  touch  with  contractors,  miners,
bridge  erectors,  freight  handlers  on  ships,  docks,  etc.,  resulting
in  a  machine  well  adapted  to  the  requirements.

There  are  a  number  of  terms  relating  to  hoists  which  are
here  given  and  which  will  be  found  helpful  in  understanding  the
subject.

Aerial  dump. — An  improved  type  of  conveyor  having  low  sides,  and
open  at  one  end.  The  form  facilitates  the  handling  of  material  by  a  cable-
way.

Boom  fall  rope. — The  rope  which  operates  the  vertical  movement  of
the  boom  of  a  derrick.

Boom  swinging  gear. — A  device  for  operating  a  radial  or  boom  swing-
ing derrick.  It  consists  of  a  drum,  or  two  spools  on  which  the  boom
swinging  ropes  are  simultaneously  wound  and  unwound.

Bull  wheel. — On  radial  derricks,  a  large  wheel  at  the  base  of  the  derrick
consisting  of  a  circular  flange  made  of  structural  T  iron  to  which  the  boom
swinging  ropes  are  attached.

4,182  Gas  and  Electric  Hoists

Clam  shell  grab. — A  term  used  to  describe  a  form  of  bucket  used  in
dredging,  excavating,  and  conveying  loose  material  such  as  coke,  sand,  etc.

Cone.— An  iron  cone  shaped  cap  used  in  logging  operations  to  assist
the  log  to  pass  over  obstacles  when  skidding.

Derrick  swinging  ropes. — The  two  ropes  which  control  the  radial  posi-
tion of  the  derrick,  or  by  means  of  which  the  derrick  is  made  to  revolve,

Differential  brakes.— Band  brakes,  in  which  both  ends  of  the  band  are
pivoted  to  the  rocker  but  at  different  distances  from  the  center  of  the
load  held,  tending  to  turn  the  rocker  in  the  direction  to  apply  the  brake.

Dock  wheels. — Small  cast  iron  wheels  fitted  to  hoists  and  forming  a
running  gear,  for  use  on  docks  or  places  where  it  is  necessary  to  move  the
hoist  frequently.

Drum. — A  revolving  flanged  cylinder,  to  which  the  hoisting  rope  is
attached,

Drum  spring. — A  spiral  steel  spring  which  pushes  the  drum  out  of
contact  with  the  friction  woods  when  the  friction  pressure  is  released  by
the  friction  lever.

Dumping  block. — A  form  of  block  consisting  of  a  small  and  large
wheel.  A  piece  of  chain  inserted  in  the  hoisting  rope  at  the  right  point
causes  the  small  wheel  to  revolve  which  raises  the  back  end  of  the  skip,
and  allows  the  load  to  dump.

Foot  brake. — A  band  brake  for  controlling  the  movement  of  the  drum.

Fixed  drum. — A  type  of  drum  in  use  where  only  single  loads  have  to
be  handled.

Friction  lever  latch. — A  device  consisting  of  a  thumb  latch  and  de-
tent, engaging  with  serrated  teeth  in  a  quadrant,  by  which  the  lever  is
held  in  any  desired  position.

Friction  nut. — A  large  nut  at  the  end  of  the  drum  shaft  and  attached
to  the  side  stand.  The  friction  lever  screw  works  in  this  nut  and  com-
municates motion  to  the  friction  pin.

Friction  pin.— A  steel  pin  which  communicates  lateral  motion  from
the  friction  screw  to  the  drum,  bringing  the  latter  into  contact  with  the
friction  woods.

Friction  screw. — A  powerful  steel  screw  located  near  the  end  of  the
drum  shaft  and  operated  by  the  friction  lever.  It  communicates  motion
to  the  friction  Din.

Gas  and  Electric  Hoists 4,183

Friction  woods. — A  concentric  layer  of  wooden  blocks  attached  to  the
gear  wheel,  at  the  end  of  the  drum,  and  furnishing  the  frictional  contact
between  the  two.

Guard  bands.— Protecting  wrought  iron  bands  fitted  over  the  gear
wheels  to  prevent  the  rope  or  any  obstruction  getting  in  the  teeth  of  the
gear.

Locking  levers. — On  double  friction  drum  engines  designed  for  han-
dling a  boom  derrick.  When  the  friction  lever  is  moved,  for  the  purpose
of  throwing  the  drum  into  gear,  the  pin  on  the  end  of  the  friction  lever
presses  against  the  curved  part  of  the  oscillating  lever  and  releases  the
foot  brake.  When  the  lever  is  moved  back,  the  operator  places  his  foot
on  the  foot  brake  for  a  moment,  and  the  oscillating  lever  drops  back  into
its  original  position,  thereby  locking  the  brake  on  the  drum.

Loose  drum. — A  drum,  free  to  revolve  on  its  shaft,  and  which  is  thrown
in  or  out  of  engagement  with  the  driving  shaft  by  means  of  a  clutch.

Main  hoist  rope. — The  rope  which  raises  or  lowers  the  load.

Operating  levers. — Devices  for  operating  the  brake,  friction,  etc.,  in
hoisting.  They  are  usually  assembled  together  and  conveniently  located
for  the  engineer.

Orange  peel  grab. — A  term  used  to  describe  a  dredging  device  which
when  opened  assumes  the  shape  of  an  orange  peel  divided  into  four  parts.

Out  haul  rope. — In  logging  operations,  an  auxiliary  rope,  worked  by  a
separate  drum.  It  is  used  to  carry  the  skidding  rope  out  into  the  woods,
which,  by  former  methods  had  to  be  done  with  mules  or  men.

Post  brakes. — A  type  of  brake  in  use  on  reversible,  mining,  and  hauling
engines.

Radial  ribs. — Cooling  ribs  placed  outside  of  the  drum  friction  surfaces
to  assist  in  carrying  off  the  heat  generated.

Shrouded  ratchet  ring. — A  ring  having  ratchet  teeth  around  its  cir-
cumference and  bolted  to  the  drum  flange.  The  outer  edge  of  the  ring  is
provided  with  a  shrouded  flange,  thereby  preventing  the  teeth  bein^r
broken  by  the  pawl,  or  the  pawl  slipping  off.

Side  stands. — Supporting  frames  usually  of  T  shaped  section  and  de-
signed to  carry  both  the  drum  and  crank  shaft  bearings.

Skip. — An  iron  box  with  a  bale  holding  from  one  to  ten  tons  of  ore  or
rock;  used  for  hoisting  and  running  between  guides,  or  in  inclined  shafts
to  run  on  a  track.

4,184

Sluing  drums. — Two  pony  drums  located  on  one  shaft,  and  especially
adapted  for  the  operation  of  radial  or  swinging  boom  derricks.

Snaking  and  loading  machine. — In  logging,  a  hoist  with  rigging  so
arranged  that  the  operations  of  hauling  and  loading  may  be  carried  on
from  separate  booms  independently  and  at  the  same  time.

Stiff  leg  derrick. — A  form  of  derrick  fitted  with  wooden  braces  instead
of  guy  ropes  for  support.

ENDLESS  HAND  CH/MN
BEARING

FlG.  6,848. — Eiementary  worm  gear  hoist.  The  hand  chain  A,  is  used  for  rotating  the  drive
pulley  B,  which  is  connected  to  the  worm  by  a  shaft.  The  worm  meshes  with  the  worm  wheel
E,  whose  shait  is  attached  to  a  pulley  over  which  passes  the  lifting  chain  G.  The  pulley  F,
has  pockets  for  receiving  the  chain  links  so  that  the  chain  cannot  slip  in  lifting  the  load  H.

Tail  sheave. — A  pulley  used  in  the  "pull  boat  system"  of  logging.

Winch  heads. — A  short  hoisting  drum  or  spool  having  curved  flanges,
and  attached  to  an  extension  of  the  drum  shaft.

4,185

Classes  of  Hoists. — The  great  variety  of  hoisting  work,
calls  for  different  kinds  of  engine  designed  to  meet  special
conditions.  They  may  be  classified  in  several  ways:

1.  With  respect  to  the  power,  as

a.  "Gas";

b.  Electric.

2.  With  respect  to  the  transmission  of  power;  as,

a.  Direct  drive;

b.  Geared  transmission.

FIGS.  6,849  and  6,850. — Chinese  windlass  illustrating  the  principle  of  the  differential  hoist.
It  consists  of  two  drums,  A  and  B,  (one  a  little  larger  than  the  other)  connected  to  a  shaft
and  having  the  ends  of  a  lifting  cable  attached  to  the  drum  as  shown,  so  that  in  turning  the
crank  the  cable  will  simultaneously  unwind  on  one  drum  and  wind  on  the  other.  Fig.  6,849
shows  the  beginning  of  the  lifting  operation.  As  the  crane  is  turned  clockwise  the  cable  winds
on  B,  and  unwinds  on  A,  and  since  B,  is  larger  in  diameter,  the  length  of  cable  between  the
two  drums  and  load  is  gradually  taken  up,  thus  lifting  the  load.  Evidently  by  making  the
difference  in  diameter  of  the  two  drums  very  small  an  extremely  large  leverage  is  obtained
thus  enabling  very  heavy  weights  to  be  lifted  with  little  effort.  The  load  will  remain  sus-
pended at  any  point,  because  the  diflerence  in  the  diameter  of  the  two  drums  is  too  small  to
overbalance  the  friction  of  the  parts.  Fig.  6,850  shows  the  end  of  the  lifting  operation.

3.  With  respect  to  the  drum  arrangement;  as,

a.  Fixed  drum;

b.  Loose  drum;

c.  Single  drum;

d.  Multi-drum;
e*  Cone  drum.

4,186

hauling  cars  or  hoisting  elevators.  It  is  found  also  on  docks  for  hoisting  and  hauling  of  gen*
eral  cargo  into  warehouses,  and  in  general  building  construction.  It  is  equipped  with  slip
ring  motor  and  drum  controller,  but  for  many  requirements  the  Jess  expensive  half  higii

torque  motor  which  has  no
controller  is  as  well  suited.

The  Simple  Hoist. — In  its  simplest  form  a  hoist  consists  of
a  hoisting  drum,  having  a  friction  brake  and  geared  to  a  motor
as  in  fig.  6,851  or  to  a  gas  engine,  as  in  fig.  6,852,  the  assembly
being  mounted  on  one  base.

f  re.  6,853. — Mead-Morrison  arrangement  of  frictions  and  brake  for  gasoline  or  electric  hoists.

A  friction  brake  is  provided  so  that  the  drum  may  be  prevented  turning
when  desired.  The  power  of  the  engine  is  applied  in  hoisting  by  means  of
a  cable,  fastened  to  the  drum;  the  cable  is  wound  upon  the  drum  in  hoist-
ing the  load,  and  unwound  when  it  is  lowered.

Drums.— Of  the  many  types  of  drum  there  are  two  general
classes:  the  fixed,  and  the  loose  drum.  The  fixed  drum  is  found
on  some  of  the  simpler  machines  designed  for  that  class  of  work

which  requires  only  the  operations  ot  lifting  or  lowering,  or
where  only  single  loads  have  to  be  handled.
The  loose  drum  is  the  type  mostly  used  and  is  so  constructed

friction  flange;  D,  drum;  E  and  F,  friction  rings;  H,  winch  head  or  spool;  J,  J,  bearings;
K,  gear  wheel;  P,  thrust  screw;  S,  drum  flange;  T,  ratchet  teeth.

in  turn  pushes  the  collar  key,  collar,  and  drum  in  the  same  direction  causing  the  latter
to  engage  with  the  friction  blocks.

that  it  may  be  thrown  in,  and  out  of  engagement  while  the
engine  is  in  motion,  by  the  action  of  friction  rings.
Fig.  6,854  shows  the  principle  of  operation.

The  drum  D,  is  thrown  into  gear  by  a  slight  endwise  movement  on  its
shaft  A,  produced  by  turning  a  lever  attached  to  the  screw  P,  whose  end

the  thrust  pin  against  a  collar  key,  which  works  in  a  slot  cut  through  the

PIGS.  6,856  to  6,862. — Loose  drum  and  friction  control  before  assembly.  The  parts  are:  A,
shaft;  B,  friction  blocks;  C,  block  retainers;  D,  drum;  E  and  F,  friction  rings  forming  a  V
groove  on  drum  flange;  H,  winch  head  or  spool;  I,  collar;  J,  thrust  collar;  L,  thrust  pin;

shaft.  The  collar  key,  together  with  the  collar,  is,  in  turn,  pushed  against
the  drum,  forcing  it  into  contact  with  friction  blocks  to  prevent  any  ro-
tation on  the  shaft.  These  blocks  consist  of  sections  of  hard  wood  B,
bolted  to  the  gear  wheel  K,  which  is  keyed  to  the  shaft.  The  adjacent
flange  on  the  drum  has  inclined  rings  E,  F,  which  register  with  the  bevel
on  the  friction  blocks,  the  endwise  movement  given  the  drum  by  turn-
ing the  friction  lever,  bringing  the  rings  into  frictional  contact  with  the
blocks,  thus  preventing  the  drum  turning  on  the  shaft  since  the  latter  is
keyed  to  the  gear  wheel  K.

When  the  friction  lever  is  turned  clockwise  to  release  the  drum,  the
latter  is  pushed  to  the  right  out  of  engagement  with  the  friction  blocks  by
the  spring  M,  allowing  the  drum  to  revolve  freely  on  the  shaft.  The  loose
drum  and  parts  are  before  being  assembled  shown  in  figs.  6,856  to  6,862.

4,190

Loose  drums  are  usually  fitted  with  band  brakes  in  order  to
reduce  the  wear  on  the  friction  blocks.  This  is  especially  de-
sirable for  long  descents,  as  in  tall  office  building  construction,
the  wear  and  heat  generated  being  excessive.

Sometimes  radial  ribs  are  placed  at  the  friction  end  of  the  drum  to  assist
in  dissipating  the  heat,  by  presenting  additional  surface  for  radiation,  and
inducing  air  currents.

FIG.  6,863.  —  Fairbanks-Morse  hopper  cooled  gas  engine  driven  hoist.  Ignition  is  by  high
tension  magneto  built  in  as  a  part  of  the  engine  itself  and  is  mounted  over  the  cam  gear
from  which  it  is  operated.

The  pile  driver  illustrates  a  class  of  work  in  which  the  loose  drum  is
most  valuable.  Instead  of  loosening  the  monkey  from  the  rope  and  having
to  re-attach  the  two  before  the  monkey  can  be  lifted  again,  the  rope  re-
mains permanently  fastened,  and  the  monkey,  in  falling,  simply  unwinds

Gras  and  Electric  Hoists

4,191

the  rope,  the  drum  revolving  on  the  shaft  in  a  reverse  direction,  and  being
again  thrown  into  gear  after  the  blow.

Foot  Brakes. — When  there  is  a  winch  head  or  spool,  as  H,
fig.  6,854,  it  is  necessary  to  have  a  band  brake  if  the  spool  is
to  be  used  at  the  same  time  that  the  load  is  hanging  on  the
drum.  In  fig.  6,864  is  shown  the  usual  type  of  brake  which
consists  of  a  metal  band  lined  with  hard-wood  segments,  and
embracing  the  external  circumference  of  the  drum  flange.

The  brake  is  operated  by  the  foot,  and,  as  shown,  is  of  the  differential

type  in  which  both  ends  of  the  band
are  pivoted  to  the  rocker,  but  at  dif-
ferent distances  from  the  center  of
the  brake  shaft,  so  that  the  strain
brought  by  the  load  held,  tends  to
turn  the  rocker  in  the  direction  to
tighten  the  brake.  By  crossing  the
ends  of  the  band  at  the  rocker,  the
rotation  of  the  brake  shaft  is  limited,
so  that  the  foot  lever,  when  released,
cannot  be  lifted  by  its  counterweight
above  a  fixed  and  convenient  posi-
tion.

Double  Drum  Boom  Swing-
ing Hoist. — The  multiplication
of  drums  is  a  feature  in  the  de-
velopment of  the  hoisting  en-
gine, adapting  it  to  service  of  a
universal  character,  such  as  the
working  of  a  radial  derrick
where  frequently  several  opera-
tions have  to  be  performed  at
the  same  time.  Fig.  6,865  il-
lustrates a  steam  hoist  adapted
to  this  class  of  work.

FIG.  6, 864 .—Band  brake  for  holding  the
drum  with  load  when  disengaged  from
the  friction.  The  metal  band  lined  with
hardwood  segments  tightly  embraces
the  circumference  of  the  drum  when
the  brake  lever  is  pressed  down.

4,192

The  forward  drum  is  for  hoisting  the  load,  and  the  other  for  raising  the
boom.  Between  the  drums  and  winch  heads  are  two  spools  for  the  derrick
swinging  ropes.  With  this  arrangement,  the  operations  of  hoisting  the
load,  raising  and  swinging  the  boom  can  go  on  simultaneously.

The  winch  heads  are  secured  to  the  shaft  by  a  sliding  key  having  a  sleeve
upon  which  the  swinging  spools  revolve.

PIG.  6,865. — Double  drum  steam  hoist  with  boom  swinging  gear  for  operating  a  radial  derrick.
The  machine  is  so  constructed  that  the  operations  of  hoisting  the  load,  raising  and  swinging
the  boom  may  go  on  simultaneously.

Friction  adequate  for  tiie  full  power  of  the  engine  is  used  between  the
swinging  spools  and  the  winch  heads.  The  frictional  contact  is  produced  by
cams  placed  between  the  swinging  drums  and  the  frame  of  the  engine;  on
the  other  side  are  anti-friction  collars  to  take  up  the  thrust.

The  operating  cams  are  connected  by  links  to  the  end  of  a  lever,  which
is  carried  on  a  shaft  extending  across  to  the  side  where  the  engineer  stands,
and  where  the  vertical  detent  lever  for  operating  the  swinging  device  is
placed.

4,193

In  operation,  a  rope  is  wound  on  the  bull  ring  of  the  derrick
and  secured  to  the  same  in  the  center,  as  shown  in  fig.  6,866.
Each  of  the  two  ends  is  fastened  to  one  of  the  two  swinging
spools,  enough  rope  being  wound  on  each  to  give  the  required
amount  of  motion  to  the  bull  wheel.  By  throwing  the  lever
forward,  it  engages  the  friction  clutch  on  that  winch,  causing
it  to  rotate  and  wind  in  the  rope,  which  turns  the  boom  in  one
direction,  the  other  winch  meanwhile  paying  out  the  rope.

### ROPES

H015TIN6  ROPES

FXG.  6,866. — Base  of  a  radial  derrick,  showing  the  bull  wheel  and  boom  swinging  ropes,  also
the  ropes  for  hoisting  and  raising  the  boom.

The  construction  of  the  cams  permits  this,  as  the  same  movement  which
engages  one  friction,  disengages  the  other.  By  throwing  the  lever  back-
ward, the  friction  of  the  rear  spool  is  engaged,  and  the  forward  spool
released.  Thus,  the  boom  can  be  turned  in  either  direction.

The  cams  operating  the  frictions  are  so  constructed  that  when  the  op-
erating lever  is  in  its  central  position,  there  is  enough  frictionat  contact
in  the  swinging  spools  to  keep  the  ropes  taut;  therefore,  when  one  spool  is

4,194

winding  in  the  swinging  rope,  the  other  has  sufficient  friction  to  prevent
the  rope,  which  is  paying  out,  overrunning.

The  brake  and  friction  levers  for  the  drums  are  shown  In  figs.  6,867
and  6,868.

tension piece  I  with  a  pin,  which  moves  upon  the  curved  surface  of  an
oscillating  lever  C.  The  latter  has  a  notch  at  the  lower  end,  engaging

FIG.  6,867.— -Lidgerwood  brake  and  friction  levers  with  safety  attachment.  When  the  friction
lever  A,  is  moved  to  throw  the  drum  into  gear,  the  pin  I,  on  a  projection  of  the  friction  lever
presses  against  the  curved  part  of  the  oscillating  lever  C,  and  releases  the  foot  brake.

with  a  pin  extending  from  the  side  of  the  foot  brake  lever.  When  the
friction  lever  is  moved,  for  the  purpose  of  throwing  the  drum  into  gear,  the
pin  on  the  end  of  the  friction  lever  presses  against  the  curved  part  of  the
oscillating  lever  and  releases  the  foot  brake.

The  friction  lever  of  the  front  drum,  as  shown  in  fig.  6,868,  is  arranged
with  a  thumb  latch  and  detent  engaging  with  serrated  teeth  in  a  quadrant

Gas  and  Electric  Hoists 4,195

by  which  the  lever  is  held  in  any  desired  position.  The  drum  is  thus
securely  locked,  so  that  hoisting  may  go  on  without  any  further  attention
to  this  lever.

When  the  lever  is  moved  back,  the  engineer  places  his  foot  on  the  foot
brake  lever  for  a  moment  and  the  oscillating  lever  drops  back  into  its
original  position,  engaging  with  the  pin  on  the  foot  brake  lever,  and  thereby
locking  the  brake  on  the  drum.  It  will  be  seen  that  this  drum,  with  the
boom  hanging  from  it,  is  locked  fast  by  the  brake,  the  pawl  being  usually
applied  as  an  additional  safeguard.

Fie.  6,868. — Lidgerwood  friction  lever  on  hoisting  drum;  a  latch  and  quadrant  hold  the  drum
nny  position.

Directions  for  Operating. — The  engineer  first  throws  in  the
front  friction  drum  lever  sufficiently  to  hoist  the  load.  He  is
then  free  to  handle  the  boom,  either  raising  or  lowering  it  with
the  rear  drum.

When  lowering  the  boom,  he  should  do  it  both  by  the  friction  lever,
and  a  slight  pressure  on  the  foot  brake.  This  prevents  the  oscillating
lever  falling  into  place  and  locking  the  brake;  it  also  allows  control  over
the  lowering  speed  of  the  boom,  and  as  soon  as  it  is  lowered  far  enough,

full  pressure  is  put  on  the  brake  lever,  and  the  friction  lever  thrown
out,  when  the  oscillating  lever  falls  into  place  and  locks  the  brake  fast.
This  use  of  the  two  levers  in  combination  for  lowering  the  boom  provides
against  the  possibility  of  the  foot  slipping  off  the  foot  brake.

It  is  important  that  the  engineer  keep  the  safety  lever  locked  to  the
foot  brake,  and  also  the  pawl  applied  at  all  times  when  the  boom  drum
is  not  in  use.  The  only  exception  to  this  rule  is  when  working  with  a
short  boom  and  the  time  required  to  throw  in  the  pawl  is  too  valuable  to
lose.  In  such  cases,  the  safety  lever  locked  to  the  foot  brake  performs
all  that  is  required  of  the  pawl  and  ratchet.

CLUTCH  LEVE.*

THROTTLE  LEV£R

FIG.  6,869. — Novo  double  drum  gas  engine  hoist  powered  with  four  cylinder  radiator  cooled
engine.  It  will  handle  two  large  material  elevators.  Some  contractors  have  this  hoist
fitted  with  an  18  in.  or  22  in.  diameter  drum  to  handle  concrete  tower  bucket  with  high
speed  and  a  standard  drum  to  handle  "Chicago"  derrick  or  miscellaneous  steel  or  platform

The  use  of  the  safety  lever  locked  to  the  foot  brake  is  desirable  with  a
long  springy  boom,  in  chaining  or  dogging  rock  after  a  blast,  at  which
time  it  may  not  be  known  whether  the  rock,  which  it  is  intended  to  hoist,
is  free.

The  effect  of  applying  the  strain  for  hoisting  the  rock  is  to  spring  the

Gas  and  Electric  Hoists  4,197

boom,  and  if  the  dogs  fly  loose,  or  the  chain  break,  the  boom  springs  back
to  its  normal  position,  slackening  for  an  instant  the  boom  fall  rope,  which
may  throw  out  the  pawl  causing  the  boom  to  fall.

Hoisting  for  Deep  Mines. — When  mining  is  to  be  done  at
great  depths,  the  problem  of  hoisting  the  ore  becomes  more
difficult  than  that  met  with  in  ordinary  mines.  For  instance,
the  variable  dead  weight  of  the  rope  must  be  taken  into  ac-
count, that  is,  when  the  cage  is  at  the  bottom,  not  only  is  its
weight,  and  that  of  the  one  to  be  hoisted,  but  also  the  full
weight  of  the  rope,  which  diminishes  as  the  cage  ascends.

FIG.  6,870. — Novo  automatic  set  solenoid  brake.

The  rope  on  the  descending  side  becomes  heavier  as  the  cage  goes  down,
with  the  result  that  it  takes  a  great  effort  for  the  engine  to  start  the  cage
or  skip  from  the  bottom,  while  as  the  ascending  cage  nears  the  top,  the
descending  cage  and  rope  may  even  more  than  balance  the  total  weight
of  the  former,  so  that  the  engine  has  no  work  to  do  during  that  period.
This  variable  load,  of  course,  results  in  poor  economy,  and  prevents  the
use  of  compound  engines.  The  difficulty  is  overcome  by  means  of  a  tail
rope  of  the  same  size  and  weight  per  foot  as  the  hoisting  rope.

The  two  ends  of  the  tail  rope  are  fastened  to  the  bottoms  of  the  two  skips
and  at  the  bottom  of  the  shaft  the  rope  passes  around  a  sheave  placed
in  guides,  or  on  a  carriage,  according  to  circumstances.  This  arrangement

4,198  Gas  and  Electric  Hoists

balances  the  hoisting  rope,  and  the  load  on  the  engine  is,  therefore,  at
all  times  only  the  weight  of  the  ore  that  is  being  hoisted.  This  counter-
balancing is  desirable,  aside  from  the  smoothness  of  operating,  because
the  engine  can  be  better  proportioned  for  the  work,  resulting  in  greater
economy.

Cone  Drum  Hoisting  Engines. — A  development  of  the  single
cylindrical  drum  with  two  ropes  is  the  double  cone  drum  as
shown  in  fig.  6,871;  this  is  another  way  of  counterbalancing
the  variable  weight  of  the  hoisting  rope.  The  theory  oi'  this

FIG.  6,871. — Allis-Chalmers  cone  drum.  A  development  of  the  single  cylindrical  drum  with
two  ropes  is  the  double  cone  drum.  This  is  another  way  of  counter  balancing  the  variable
weight  of  the  hoisting  rope.  The  theory  of  this  construction  is  that  as  the  weight  of  the
rope  increases,  the  radius  at  which  it  acts  correspondingly  decreases,  thus  equalizing  the
work  of  the  engine.

construction  is  that  as  the  weight  of  the  rope  increases,  the
radius  at  which  it  acts  correspondingly  decreases,  thus  equaliz-
ing the  work  of  the  engine.

The  dimensions  of  the  drum  must  be  proportioned  for  each  particular
case,  and  if  used  under  different  circumstances,  the  drum  would  fail  to
fulfill  the  purpose  of  its  design.

Gas  and  Electric  Hoists  4,199

For  very  deep  hoisting  in  order  to  balance  the  great  weight  of  rope,
the  central  portion  of  the  drum  would  become  inconveniently  large  in
diameter,  therefore  this  portion  is  made  cylindrical,  and  is  used  by  both
ropes.  This  arrangement,  while  it  shortens  the  drum,  makes  it  impossible
to  balance  the  weight  of  rope.

Reel  Hoisting  Engines*— The  reel  hoist  is  often  used  where  it
is  not  the  intention  to  hoist  always  in  balance,  and  where  no  tail
rope  or  compensating  device  such  as  a  conical  drum  can  be  used.

The  greatest  weight  to  be  lifted  by  any  hoist  is  when  the
loaded  cage  is  at  the  bottom  of  the  shaft,  consequently  all  the
rope  is  off  the  drum  or  reel.  At  this  point,  however,  the  reel
begins  to  wind  on  its  shortest  radius.  As  the  rope  winds  on  the
reel,  the  total  load  decreases  while  the  leverage  of  the  rope  on
the  reel  increases,  thus  keeping  the  load  on  the  engine  nearly
uniform  while  lifting  one  cage  unbalanced.

Moreover,  the  rope  always  leads  straight  to  the  head  sheave  instead
of  at  a  considerable  angle  as  occurs  when  winding  on  a  drum.  This  makes
a  compact  arrangement  which  is  an  advantage  in  some  cases.

Reel  hoists  are  used  mostly  in  the  western  part  of  America.  As  a  rule,
hoisting  is  done  there  from  different  levels,  one  car  often  being  hoisted
from  one  level  while  the  car  on  the  other  deck  may  come  from  another
level;  under  these  conditions  the  hoisting  cannot  be  done  in  balance.

Capacity  of  Hoisting  Engines. — The  horse  power  required
to  raise  a  load  at  a  given  speed  is  equal  to

gross  weight  in  Ibs.X  speed  in  feet  per  minute
33^000

To  this  there  should  be  added  from  25  to  50  per  cent  for  friction,  con-
tingencies, etc.  The  gross  weight  includes  the  weight  of  the  cage,  load,
and  rope.  In  a  shaft  with  two  cages  balancing  each  thus,  the  net  load  is
taken.

Limit  of  Depth  in  Hoisting. — Taking  the  weight  of  a  hoist-
ing rope,  \y%  inches  in  diameter,  at  two  pounds  per  foot,  and
its  breaking  strength  at  84,000  Ibs.,  it  should,  theoretically,

4,200

Gfas  and  Electric  Hoists

sustain  itself  until  42,000  feet  long  before  breaking  from  its  own
weight.  But  taking  the  usual  factor  of  safety  of  7,  then  the  safe
working  length  of  such  a  rope  would  be  only

42,000 -f- 7 =6,000  feet.

If  now  a  weight  of  three  tons,  which  is  equivalent  to  that
of  a  cage  of  moderate  capacity  with  its  loaded  cars,  be  hung

FIG.  6,872.  —  Mead-Morrison  slack  line  hoist  designed  for  slack  line  excavating.  The  motor
is  a  continuous  running,  specially  wound  type  that  builds  up  torque  as  the  load  increases.

The  limit  may  be  considerably  increased,  by  using:  1,  special
steel  rope  of  greater  strength;  2,  a  smaller  factor  of  safety,  and
3,  taper  ropes.

Strength  of  Wire  Rope  4,201

## CHAPTER  159A

Strength  of  Wire  Rope

Wire  rope  in  service  subjected  to  several  kinds  of  stresses.
The  stresses  most  frequently  encountered  are  direct  tension,
stress  due  to  acceleration,  stress  due  to  sudden  or  shock  loads,
stress  due  to  bending,  and  stress  resulting  from  several  forces
acting  at  one  time.

Since  the  strength  of  a  wire  rope  is  determined  by  its  size,
grade  and  construction,  these  three  factors  should  be  considered.

Factors  of  Safety.— By  definition,  the  factor  of  safety  is:
The  ratio  of  the  strength  of  the  rope  to  the  working  load.  Thus,  a
wire  rope  with  a  strength  of  10,000  pounds  and  a  total  working
load  of  2,000  pounds  would  be  operating  with  a  safety  factor  of
five.  Some  factors  of  safety  are  given  in  the  following  table:

### MINIMUM  SAFETY  FACTORS

Type  of  Service  Minimum  Safety  Factor

Miscellaneous  hoisting  equipment .  .  5.0

Haulage  ropes 6.00

Overhead  and  gantry  cranes 6.00

Jib  and  Pillar  cranes 6.0

4,202 Strength  of  Wire  Rope

### MINIMUM  SAFETY  FACTORS

Table — continued

Derricks 6.0

Small  electric  and  air  hoists 7.0

Hot  ladle  cranes 8.0

Slings 8.0

The  table  on  next  page  gives  the  safe  loads  and  weights  of
wire  rope.

Maximum  Load  on  a  Wire  Rope.— The  method  of  calcu-
lation here  given  is  not  intended  for  very  close  calculations,  and
should  not  be  used  where  the  rope  is  subjected  to  sudden  jerks,
It  will  serve  as  a  guide,  however,  to  show  whether  or  not  a  rope
is  overloaded.  For  short  periods  of  time,  steam  engines  and
electric  motors  may  be  operated  with  -high  overloads  such  as
25%  for  steam  and  50%  for  electricity.  Knowing  the  horse
power  of  the  prime  mover  provides  a  basis  for  this  calculation.

When  hard  pulling  is  required,  the  overload  values  mentioned
should  be  added  to  the  horse  power  rating.

One  horse  power  is  the  equivalent  of

550  ft.  Ibs.  of  work  per  second;

therefore,  the  total  horse  power  exerted  by  a  steam  engine  or
electric  motor  in  pulling  a  rope  may  be  expressed:

Horse  power  =  feet  X  P°unds
seconds  X  550

JLJLVSX£>^    J^VfVVC'JL       '  — T

seconds  X  550

where  feet  represents  the  number  of  feet  of  rope  wound  on  the
drum  in  the  measured  time.

4,202B  Strength  of  Wire  Rope

By  rearrangement:

Pull  on  rope  in  pounds  =  Horse  power  X  seconds  X  550

feet

There  are  now  on  the  right  hand  side  of  the  equation  two  un-
known factors,  time  and  distance.  By  placing  a  marker  on  the
rope  or  watching  the  end  connection,  it  is  very  easy  with  the
aid  of  a  watch  and  a  rule,  to  determine  very  closely  what  these
factors  actually  are.

The  time  at  which  these  observations  are  taken  should  be
when  the  engine  or  motor  is  pulling  the  hardest,  as  indicated  by
sound  and  the  slowing  down  of  speed.  If  the  equipment  be
stalled  by  excessive  loads,  these  observations  should  be  made
just  before  motion  ceases.

Example:  A  slack  line  excavator  driven  by  a  25  H.P.  electric  motor  is
digging  in  very  hard  ground.  When  the  bucket  strikes  the  hard  places,  it
digs  15  feet  in  10  seconds.  What  is  the  pull  on  the  line?

By  using  the  formula  we  find:

Pull  on  rope  in  pounds  =  2SX1.6X10X6SO  _  ^760 1*.

where  the  factor  1.5  in  the  foregoing  represents  the  50%  overload  on  the

Providing  a  safety  factor  of  five  for  the  rope  on  this  installation  requires
a  rope  with  a  strength  of  5  X  13,750  «  68,750  Ibs.

Reserve  Strength.— The  reserve  strength  of  a  wire  rope  is
the  strength  of  the  rope  exclusive  of  the  outer  wires,  which  are
the  first  to  be  destroyed  by  wear  and  abrasion.

As  the  number  of  layers  of  wires  in  each  strand  increases,  the
reserve  strength  increases.

Well  lubricated  ropes  have  the  following  approximate  reserve
strength  in  terms  of  total  strengths  of  new  ropes.

4,202D  Strength  of  Wire  Rope

How  to  Find  the  Pull  Required  to  Lift  a  Given  Weight

When  the  weight  to  be  lifted  and  the  number  of  parts  at  the  movable  block
are  known,  divide  the  weight  by  the  number  of  parts  at  the  movable  block
to  get  the  mechanical  advantage  or  necessary  pull  to  lift  the  weight,  dis-
regarding any  loss  by  friction.  There  is  always  a  friction  loss  around
sheaves,  and  a  rule  which  is  safe,  but  only  approximate,  is  to  allow  107o
friction  loss  for  each  sheave  that  is  3"  or  more  in  diameter.  Add  this  frac*
tional  loss  to  the  load,  and  proceed  as  if  friction  were  not  present,

Example

How  much  pull  will  be  required  to  lift  100  Ibs.  with  1  single  and  1
double  block?  The  tackle  consists  of  sheaves  and  3  supporting  parts,  or  has  3
mechanical  advantage  of  3.  Allow  10%  friction  loss  for  each  sheave*

Solution:

3  x  10%  =  30%  =  Total  amount  of  friction.

30%  of  100  Ibs.  =  30  Ibs.

100  Ibs,  +  30  Ibs.  =  130  Ibs.  =  Total  amount  to  be  lifted.

13°3lbs'  =  43.3  Ibs.  ==  the  pull  required  to  lift  100  Ibs.
o

A  more  accurate  computation  is  as  follows:
100  Ibs.

3x.90x.90x.90

=  45.75  Ibs.

The  factor  .90  represents  the  90%  efficiency  of  each  sheave  and  should
appear  in  the  denominator  of  the  fraction  as  many  times  as  there  are
sheaves  in  the  tackle*

## CHAPTER  160

Any  appliance  for  lifting  and  moving  loads  is  far  superior
to  a  gang  of  laborers,  especially  so  in  the  case  of  very  heavy
loads.

FIG.  6,874,-Euclid  crane  construction  1.  Double  web  bov  girder  bridges.  The  girders  are
reinforced  by  means  of  channel  diaphragms  at  close  intervals.  The  end  trucks  are  of  the
box  type,  made  up  of  heavy  channels  and  plates.  The  illustration  shows  the  way  the  girders
are  notched  out  and  rest  upon  the  top  of  the  end  trucks.

By  definition,  a  crane  is  a  machine  for  lifting,  lowe  ing  and
moving  a  load  in  a  horizontal  direction,  as  distinguished  from  a
hoist  which  simply  lifts  and  lowers  a  load,

The  numerous  and  diverse  conditions  of  service  require  a
multiplicity  of  types,  and  accordingly  cranes  may  be  classified:

runway.
